    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="23dfb9a81b554c5588483f7f6da109c1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '4a84a103-70a3-4560-8ee9-2879ac14e0d2'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d77521213" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Compensating for the irreversible loss of limited active sodium (Na) is crucial for enhancing the energy density of practical sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) full-cell, especially when employing hard carbon anode with initially lower coulombic efficiency. Introducing sacrificial cathode presodiation agents, particularly those that own potential anionic oxidation activity with a high theoretical capacity, can provide additional sodium sources for compensating Na loss. Herein, Ni atoms are precisely implanted at the Na sites within Na<sub>2</sub>O framework, obtaining a (Na<sub>0.89</sub>Ni<sub>0.05</sub>□<sub>0.06</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O (Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O) presodiation agent. The synergistic interaction between Na vacancies and Ni catalyst effectively tunes the band structure, forming moderate Ni–O covalent bonds, activating the oxidation activity of oxygen anion, reducing the decomposition overpotential to 2.8&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), and achieving a high presodiation capacity of 710 mAh/g<sub>≈Na2O</sub> (Na<sub>2</sub>O decomposition rate &gt;80%). Incorporating currently-modified presodiation agent with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes, the energy density of corresponding Na-ion full-cells presents an essential improvement of 23.9% and 19.3%, respectively. Further, not limited to Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O, the structure–function relationship between the anionic oxidation mechanism and electrode–electrolyte interface fabrication is revealed as a paradigm for the development of sacrificial cathode presodiation agent.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202407720-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202407720-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>At present, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) reign as the preeminent technology for energy storage in portable devices and automotive applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0001_R_d77521205e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Catal. eScience Nat. Energy">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the scarcity of lithium resources and geographical limitations makes it impossible to sustain and meet the exploding market demand.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0002_R_d77521205e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nat. Energy Sci. Bull.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering the widespread availability and cost-effectiveness of sodium resources, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) technology emerges as a compelling alternative to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0003_R_d77521205e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett. Joule Chem. Soc. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the initial cycle of SIBs, the anode side irreversibly consumes the finite sodium ions released from the cathode, resulting in a decrease in initial coulombic efficiency (ICE), and consequently, lowering energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0004_R_d77521205e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Releasing the inherent entire capacity of cathode materials across the full battery system is a complex and challenging endeavor. Especially when coupled with mainstream hard carbon (HC) anodes, their relatively lower ICE (70–90%) inevitably triggers irreversible consumption of active sodium, leading to a substantial loss in full-cell capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0004_R_d77521205e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adma202407720-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0005_R_d77521205e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This situation exacerbates for P2-phase materials with sodium deficiency structures as they struggle even more intensely to uphold their already constrained capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0006_R_d77521205e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Acc. Mater. Res. J. Power Sources J. Power Sources">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the energy density of SIBs significantly trails behind that of LIBs, rendering any slight capacity loss significantly impactful on application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0007_R_d77521205e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, the development of presodiation techniques is imperative.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0008_R_d77521205e712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Cent. Sci. J. Energy Chem.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To compensate for the initial active sodium loss and achieve high energy density in SIBs, presodiation technology has garnered considerable attention.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0009_R_d77521205e719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Electrochem. Commun. Nat. Commun.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cathode presodiation technique typically entails the introduction of sacrificial sodium-rich materials to the cathode side, thereby supplying additional active sodium sources to compensate for the irreversible Na loss on the anode side.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0010_R_d77521205e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Energy J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ideal sacrificial cathode presodiation agent should have the following characteristics: large capacity, high capacity utilization, moderate operating voltage, low cost, easy operation, and compatibility with existing production processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0011_R_d77521205e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Lab ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High capacity and high capacity utilization can significantly reduce the amount of presodiation agent used.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0012_R_d77521205e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, obtaining a presodiation agent with all these characteristics remains a huge challenge. For example, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> has a theoretical capacity of up to 505 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, but its actual capacity is only 90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, resulting in a capacity utilization rate of only 17.8%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0013_R_d77521205e749" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Mater. J. Electrochem.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, despite the close to 100% capacity utilization rate of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, its decomposition overpotential is relatively high (&gt;4.1&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), and its theoretical capacity is relatively low: ≈400 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0014_R_d77521205e765" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ChemSusChem">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The antifluorite structure of Na<sub>2</sub>O has the highest theoretical capacity (865 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and is considered one of the optimal choices for cathode presodiation agents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0015_R_d77521205e775" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, during the actual charging process, Na<sub>2</sub>O exhibits extremely high overpotential/polarization (&gt;4.5&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), making it difficult to activate oxygen-derived/related anion oxidation activity and achieve high practical capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0016_R_d77521205e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In practice, leveraging transition metal (TM) media to enhance the oxidation activity of anion has been widely applied in metal–oxygen batteries and lithium–sodium-rich cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0017_R_d77521205e791" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Chem. ACS Cent. Sci. Sci. Bull.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In metal–oxygen batteries, accelerating reaction kinetics can be achieved through the surface contact mechanism between TM and reactants.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0018_R_d77521205e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Joule Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, constrained by model limitations, rapid conversion can only occur within the contact region, posing a challenge in metal–oxygen batteries contact mechanisms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0019_R_d77521205e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this case, followed by the design principle of the cathode in metal–oxygen batteries, the noble metal catalyst in Na<sub>2</sub>O-TM cathode presodiation agent is yet limited by the surface point contact mechanism, in which Na<sub>2</sub>O cannot fully decompose (≈65% decomposition rate).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0020_R_d77521205e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, enhancing the covalent interaction between lattice oxygen and TM is crucial for activating the oxidation activity of O-related anionic redox in lithium–sodium-rich cathodes to achieve higher capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0021_R_d77521205e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Acc. Chem. Res. Chem. Rev. ACS Nano">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Na<sub>2</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub>, the redox activity of oxygen anion is activated by sintering to alter the pristine antifluorite structure of Na<sub>2</sub>O, forming Ni─O covalent interactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0022_R_d77521205e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the strong Ni─O covalency and high Ni content in Na<sub>2</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub> result in a low presodiation capacity. The optimal design approach should involve minimizing the introduction of TM; while, preserving the pristine antifluorite structure to control the band structure and activate the oxidation activity of anionic oxygen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0023_R_d77521205e838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, achieving this is currently challenging, given the existing level of understanding. Further, the oxidation pathway and intermediate states of anion remain unclear, posing unresolved issues in both metal–oxygen batteries and lithium–sodium-rich cathode materials, hindering the provision of favorable guidance for activating the oxidation activity of anionic oxygen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0024_R_d77521205e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Chem J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Simultaneously, determining the safety hazards posed by intermediate products to the full-cell system is challenging.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0025_R_d77521205e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, designing a catalytic-covalent network framework based on a clear understanding of the Na<sub>2</sub>O decomposition pathway is urgently needed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0026_R_d77521205e856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Energy">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In this study, we employed NiO as the TM source and precisely implanted Ni into the Na<sub>2</sub>O framework, synthesizing (Na<sub>0.89</sub>Ni<sub>0.05</sub>□<sub>0.06</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O (Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O) and NiO composite material (NNO), achieving a high presodiation capacity of 710 mAh/g<sub>≈Na2O</sub>. We precisely implanted Ni atoms into the Na sites of the Na<sub>2</sub>O lattice, introducing doping energy levels and Na vacancies, thereby tuning the energy level/band structure of Na<sub>2</sub>O and enhancing its conductivity. Simultaneously, in combination with the catalytic effect of the NiO surface, the cleavage of Na─O bonds and the formation of O─O bonds were accelerated, synergistically promoting the decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>O. Further, we elucidated the local structural evolution of the presodiation agent and the reaction pathways of O anion, clarifying the interaction between TM and the oxidation activity of O anion and conducting qualitative and quantitative analysis of relevant reaction products and residues. Unlike the surface-contacted mechanism (between ORR product and catalytic framework) in metal–oxygen batteries, we synergistically modified both the internal crystal structure and surface contact of the presodiation. Further, the Ni─O covalent interaction was moderate, which prevented lattice structural changes induced by strong covalent interactions, making Na<sub>2</sub>O decomposition more favorable. Finally, applying the NNO presodiation agent to HC||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) full-cell increased the capacity from 75.3 to 93.3 mAh/g<sub>NVP</sub>. Similarly, in HC||Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMO) full-cell, there was a noticeable initial discharge capacity enhancement of 12.1 mAh/g<sub>NNMO</sub>. This indicates the universality of NNO as a presodiation agent and provides a new perspective for improving the energy density of SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202407720-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202407720-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The thermodynamic decomposition potential of Na<sub>2</sub>O for the oxygen evolution reaction (OER, Na<sub>2</sub>O/O<sub>2</sub>≈4e<sup>−</sup>) is noted to be 1.95&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0015_R_d77521205e929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, in practical charging processes, Na<sub>2</sub>O exhibits a significantly elevated overpotential and proves challenging to decompose, with a mere capacity of ≈11 mAh/g<sub>≈Na2O</sub> achieved upon charging to 4.5&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202407720-fig-0001">1a</a>). To facilitate the decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>O, we endeavored to introduce various transition metal oxides (NiO, CoO, or FeO) to lower its decomposition overpotential (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Experimental results show that the strategic incorporation of NiO significantly reduces the decomposition overpotential of Na<sub>2</sub>O (from 4.5 to 2.8&nbsp;V), leading to the emergence of a distinct extended plateau at 2.8&nbsp;V, meeting the voltage criterion as a cathode presodiation agent. Moreover, as shown Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information, by controlling the optimum molar ratio of Na<sub>2</sub>O to NiO at 4:1 (resulting in a Na<sub>8</sub>NiO<sub>5</sub> stoichiometric ratio), a higher desodiation capacity is achieved compared to Na<sub>2</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub> owing to a larger Na/Ni ratio (lower Ni content). The material prepared by ball milling Na<sub>2</sub>O with NiO is called NNO presodiation agent. Notably, it can achieve an impressive capacity of 546 mAh/g<sub>≈(Na2O+NiO)</sub> (≈710 mAh/g<sub>∼Na2O</sub>), extracting ≈1.64 Na<sup>+</sup> ions, with a remarkable decomposition efficiency of up to 82% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0001">1a</a>). As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0001">1b</a>, the exceptional electrochemical performance is attributed to the perfect integration achieved between NiO and Na<sub>2</sub>O, facilitated by the high-energy ball milling process. Through the synergistic effects of mechanochemical reactions and localized heating/pressure resulting from collisions, it becomes feasible to facilitate the fragmentation of NiO and the subsequent implantation of Ni elements into the lattice of Na<sub>2</sub>O.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202407720-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/64fa8004-a6a2-4654-abea-eeeddf89691e/adma202407720-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/64fa8004-a6a2-4654-abea-eeeddf89691e/adma202407720-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/14ac81f5-45e5-4446-ab9f-e499e2b13f51/adma202407720-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/64fa8004-a6a2-4654-abea-eeeddf89691e/adma202407720-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407720-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407720" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of Na<sub>2</sub>O-based high capacity presodiation agent. a) Voltage profile for (Na<sub>2</sub>O + NiO) composite cathode at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> with a 4.5&nbsp;V upper cutoff potential; the inset shows the pure Na<sub>2</sub>O cathode capacity (<i>&lt;</i>15 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) at cutoff 4.5&nbsp;V. b) Schematic illustration of the preparation procedure of NNO composite presodiation agent by high-energy ball milling. c) sXRD pattern and d) NPD pattern of NNO presodiation agent and the results of fitting via Rietveld refinement. e) The density of states (DOS) of Na<sub>2</sub>O (top) and Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O (bottom). f) Gibbs free energy diagrams during the pure Na<sub>2</sub>O and Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O decomposition process.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) shows that the raw materials undergo mechanical pulverization to refine their particle sizes, resulting in micro- or nano-scale particles. The prepared composite materials display secondary particles comprising numerous smaller primary particles. Simultaneously, as shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information, ball milling ensures the uniform distribution of Ni elements within the material. The synchrotron X-ray diffraction (sXRD) and the Rietveld refinement results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0001">1c</a>; Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1 and S2</a>, Supporting Information indicate the (Na<sub>0.89</sub>Ni<sub>0.05</sub>□<sub>0.06</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O (Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O) has almost the same antifluorite structure as that of Na<sub>2</sub>O (Fm-3m space group); however, ≈25% Fm-3m cubic phase (NiO) also remains. The neutron powder diffraction (NPD) further confirms that Ni ions substitutionally enter Na<sup>+</sup> sites and introduce sodium vacancies, which leads to an expansion of the lattice volume and an increase in the crystal lattice parameters (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0001">1d</a>; Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3–S5</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculation is carried out to elucidate the energy band/level structure of the material with deeper insights into Na<sub>2</sub>O decomposition. The density of states (DOS) calculations of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0001">1e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information show that Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O appears with new doping energy levels (Ni 3d) and with a narrowed bandgap, conducive for the facile electron transfer and accelerating the decomposition kinetics process. The element-specified partial density of states (PDOS) indicate the state near the Fermi level was mainly dominated by 3d orbitals of Ni, and the formation of Ni─O covalent interaction, which further evidence that Ni favors enhanced electronic interactions. Further, the NiO surface exhibits a higher affinity for adsorbing Na<sub>2</sub>O, indicating the strong interaction between Na<sub>2</sub>O and NiO, thereby facilitating the catalytic decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>O (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the Gibbs free energy calculations confirm that under the same decomposition pathway (2Na<sub>2</sub>O = 4Na<sup>+</sup> + O<sub>2</sub> + 4e<sup>−</sup>), Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O composite materials possess a lower Gibbs free energy during the Na extraction stage, facilitating cleaving of the Na─O bonds. Moreover, the calculated OER potential of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O is 2.28&nbsp;V, lower than the 2.99&nbsp;V of Na<sub>2</sub>O (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0001">1f</a>). In summary, the effective implantation of Ni into Na sites and the introduction of Na vacancies tune the band structure and activate the oxidation activity of oxygen anion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The decreased Gibbs free energy required for Na<sub>2</sub>O decomposition is attributed to Ni implantation, which modifies the Na─O local structure and weakens Na─O interactions, thereby promoting its decomposition. In addition, the external catalytic effect of NiO accelerates the cleavage of Na─O bonds and the formation of O─O bonds, further contributing to the reduced decomposition overpotential of Na<sub>2</sub>O.</p>
         
         <p>The charge–discharge curves of the NNO presodiation cathode reveal a decomposition plateau at ≈2.8&nbsp;V, followed by a gradual voltage rise from 2.9 to 4.0&nbsp;V and a rapid ascent from 4.0 to 4.5&nbsp;V. Charging to 4.0&nbsp;V yields a capacity of 525&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating significant Na<sub>2</sub>O decomposition (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). After the initial charge, the coin cell is disassembled to release O<sub>2</sub>; then, reassembled for testing, removing the O<sub>2</sub> reduction plateau near ≈2.5&nbsp;V (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). To elucidate the structural evolution mechanism during the sustained Na extraction and gaseous O<sub>2</sub> release processes of the NNO presodiation agent, we perform synchrotron X-ray diffraction (sXRD) measurements at different charged states, and subsequently, analyze the residual phases. As depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202407720-fig-0002">2a</a>, the (220) diffraction peak (2<i>θ</i> = 19.8°) significantly diminishes at 4.0&nbsp;V. Upon charging to 4.5&nbsp;V, all diffraction peaks associated with Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O vanish, leaving only those corresponding to the NiO phase, signifying the substantial decomposition of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O. Upon discharge to 1.5&nbsp;V, the NiO diffraction peaks remain unaltered throughout the process, indicating the electrochemical inactivity of NiO. The decomposition of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O during the charge–discharge process within the specified voltage window is irreversible, aligning with the characteristics of a presodiation agent.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202407720-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/020e8367-f3f3-43de-b86d-578d52d6c426/adma202407720-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/020e8367-f3f3-43de-b86d-578d52d6c426/adma202407720-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/760599d9-0aa6-413e-b001-f431e02e1b79/adma202407720-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/020e8367-f3f3-43de-b86d-578d52d6c426/adma202407720-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407720-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407720" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The structural and local covalent environment evolution during NNO decomposition. a) Ex situ sXRD patterns and the results of fitting via Rietveld refinement of NNO presodiation agent in different voltages. The sXRD peak intensities of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O presodiation agent at 2<i>θ</i> = 14.0°, 19.8°, 23.3°, 24.3°, and 28.3° (Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O), 2<i>θ</i> = 16.4°, 18.9°, and 26.9° (NiO), respectively. b–d) TEM images of charged NNO presodiation agent cathode at pristine, charge 4.3&nbsp;V, and discharge 1.5&nbsp;V. Insets present the corresponding SEAD patterns. The red and yellow marker lines represent the NiO and Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O phases, respectively, and the white ones represent the amorphous phase. e) Ni K-edge X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra at different voltage states. Upper left inset: enlarged pre-edge of Ni K-edge XANES spectra and a schematic illustration of Ni–O tetrahedral (tetra.) configuration (Ni in Na<sub>2</sub>O) and octahedral (octa.) configuration (Ni in NiO). Inset below right: edge positions of Ni K-edge at different voltage states. f) Ni K-edge EXAFS spectra (weighted by k<sup>3</sup>) of pristine and charged 4.5&nbsp;V NNO presodiation agent. The Ni molar percentage pie chart of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O (green region) and NiO (purple region) in NNO presodiation agent. The fitted Ni–O coordination numbers (C.N.) are shown in the inset.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) further corroborates the evolution process observed in the sXRD spectra. Analysis of the fast Fourier transform (FFT) and selected area electron diffraction (SAED) images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0002">2b</a>) reveals lattice fringes corresponding to both Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O and NiO phases in the pristine powder. The lattice fringe of 0.202&nbsp;nm corresponds to the (220) crystal plane of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O, exhibiting a wider lattice fringe compared to the pristine Na<sub>2</sub>O (0.197&nbsp;nm), confirming that Ni substitution enlarges the interplanar spacing (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The lattice fringes of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O vanish, with only some residual amorphous phases and NiO observed at 4.5&nbsp;V. Upon discharge to 1.5&nbsp;V, only NiO is observed, indicating NiO does not undergo reversible Na ion extraction and insertion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0002">2c,d</a>).</p>
         
         <p>The X-ray absorption spectroscopy (hard-XAS) measurements conducted elucidate the local coordination environment of Ni atoms within the NNO presodiation agent across different charge–discharge states. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0002">2e</a>, the pristine state exhibits strong pre-edge and relatively low-intensity white line peak characteristics of tetrahedral coordination, confirming the successful substitution of Ni into the Na sites of Na<sub>2</sub>O, forming a geometric structure of Ni─O tetrahedra. With increasing charging depth, the gradual decomposition of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O leads to the disappearance of the Ni─O tetrahedral configuration, thereby making the features of the Ni─O octahedral configuration (NiO) more prominent. The edge position indicates that Ni is substituted into the framework of Na<sub>2</sub>O in the form of Ni<sup>2+</sup>, and there is no significant change in the valence state of Ni throughout the charge–discharge process. Extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) can provide more detailed information regarding the local TM-O/TM-TM covalent environment. Based on the molar ratio of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O and NiO phases in the NNO presodiation agent being 0.75:0.25, it can be inferred that the elemental molar ratios of Ni in Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O and NiO are 23.1% and 76.9%, respectively, with corresponding Ni─O coordination numbers of 4 and 6. The fitting results in R-space (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0002">2f</a>) reveal that the coordination number of the Ni─O shell in the pristine state is 5.5, further confirming the existence of a four-coordinated structure of Ni in Na<sub>2</sub>O. Upon charging to 4.5&nbsp;V, the decomposition of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O leads to a collapse in structure, altering the Ni─O coordination environment and reducing the overall Ni─O coordination number as ≈5.2. The introduction of Ni into Na sites effectively alters the local structure of the Na<sub>2</sub>O framework, activating the oxidative activity of O anion and accelerating the drastic structural evolution in response to active Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. This also demonstrates the catalytic role of Ni in the Na<sub>2</sub>O framework, with the irreversible capacity during decomposition attributed to the contribution of O oxidation reactions.</p>
         
         <p>To further explore the mechanism of anionic oxygen reaction during the NNO presodiation agent decomposition process (releasing active sodium source), online electrochemical mass spectrometry (OEMS) stands out as a powerful in situ characterization technique for dynamically tracking the evolution of O<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub> gases. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202407720-fig-0003">3a,b</a> depicts the correlation between voltage distribution and gas release rate, revealing distinct patterns throughout various stages of the process. During the initial phase (from open circuit voltage [OCV] to 3.45&nbsp;V), a rapid gas evolution of O<sub>2</sub> is observed, followed by a relatively stable evolution rate in the second phase (3.45 to 3.65&nbsp;V), notably exhibiting a reaction rate approaching 4e<sup>−</sup>/O<sub>2</sub>, indicative of the direct decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>O into gaseous O<sub>2</sub>. Subsequently, in the third stage (3.65 to 4.5&nbsp;V), a gradual decline followed by a precipitous drop in the rate of oxygen evolution is observed. Further, during this third stage, the emergence of CO<sub>2</sub> release is observed, stemming from the decomposition of carbonate byproducts at elevated charging potentials. Then, OEMS is used more to monitor the release of gaseous O<sub>2</sub> during cycling, and titration mass spectrometry (TMS) is used to characterize the presence of oxidized oxygen species (e.g., peroxides species) in the lattice. Thus, to corroborate the existence of peroxides (or superoxides), TMS analysis is conducted (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0003">3c</a>). During the initial stages of charging (from OCV to 3.45&nbsp;V), numerous oxidized lattice O─O dimer species (e.g., peroxides or superoxides) are trapped within the lattice; yet, to be released in the form of gaseous O<sub>2</sub> by TMS characterization. With the progression of charging depth, a greater portion of trapped O<sub>2</sub> converts into gaseous O<sub>2</sub>, resulting in a noticeable decline in subsequently titrated oxygen content. In addition, nucleophilic species attacking the electrolyte give rise to carbonate byproducts, which subsequently decompose into gaseous CO<sub>2</sub>. Notably, the observation of peroxides and gaseous O<sub>2</sub> species can clearly demonstrate the formation of O─O dimer during the activation of O anion oxidative activity in Na<sub>2</sub>O.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202407720-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/13a8c7b1-b75a-4ac5-acc0-d2ef8cebe850/adma202407720-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/13a8c7b1-b75a-4ac5-acc0-d2ef8cebe850/adma202407720-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/593b1cf1-600a-4629-bf28-13adbd42d62b/adma202407720-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/13a8c7b1-b75a-4ac5-acc0-d2ef8cebe850/adma202407720-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407720-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407720" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Analysis of oxygen behavior upon Na<sub>2</sub>O oxidation in NNO. a) The top panel shows the galvanostatic charge curve for the initial charging process of the NNO presodiation agent cathode at a current density of 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Five points are labeled in the charge curve: OCV, 3.25&nbsp;V,3.45&nbsp;V, 3.65&nbsp;V, and 4.5&nbsp;V, respectively. b) The middle panel shows OEMS results of corresponding time-resolved evolution rates for O<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub> during initial charging. c) TMS result: amounts of O<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub> collected from the NNO presodiation agent plates with different specific voltages. d) O K-edge XANES spectra of the NNO presodiation agent cathode and standard samples at TEY modes. The peak at 533&nbsp;eV represents oxygen in the antifluorite structure of Na<sub>2</sub>O; the peak at 531.4&nbsp;eV represents <i>σ</i>* (O─O) peroxide species; and the peak at 531.2&nbsp;eV represents the hybridized state of O 2p and Ni 3d orbitals in NiO. e) The schematic diagram illustrating the dynamics of electrochemical transfer oxidation process during the charging of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O. The electronic structure of Na<sub>2</sub>O exhibits a charge transfer electronic ground state in the reduced phase (left). The O (2p) lone pairs denote |O<sub>2p</sub>. The splitting of the O 2p narrow band into distinct <i>σ</i>, π, π*, and <i>σ</i>* states is illustrated by the red (Δ<i><sup>σ</sup></i><sub>O─O</sub>) and green (Δ<sup>π</sup><sub>O─O</sub>) arrows, with respect to the conversion of O─O dimer species (bottom horizontal axis).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Further, we elucidated the essence of the oxidative–reductive changes in oxygen anion through O K-edge X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectroscopy. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0003">3d</a> presents XANES spectra of NNO cathodes in different charge states, juxtaposed with spectra of NiO, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and Na<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for comparison. The spectrum of the pristine sample observed in the surface-sensitive total electron yield (TEY) mode closely resembles that of Na<sub>2</sub>O, indicating that the oxygen near the surface exists in a state akin to the antifluorite-like structure of Na<sub>2</sub>O (exhibiting a characteristic peak at 533&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0027_R_d77521205e1361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Energy">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon charging to 3.3&nbsp;V, the peak of Na<sub>2</sub>O gradually becomes broader and weaker. Simultaneously, the characteristic peak of NiO (the hybridization of O 2p and Ni 3d orbitals, ≈531.2&nbsp;eV) gradually becomes prominent, overlapping with the characteristic peak of <i>σ</i>* (O─O) peroxide species (531.4&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0028_R_d77521205e1371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nat. Energy Joule">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, when charged to 4.5&nbsp;V and discharged to 1.5&nbsp;V, the peak of Na<sub>2</sub>O completely disappears, and only the characteristic peak of NiO is observed.</p>
         
         <p>Building upon a previous report, the disparity in energy levels between the O<sub>2p</sub> band and the empty metallic band is recognized as Δct within the charge transfer mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0029_R_d77521205e1383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Mater. Nat. Commun.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Analyzing from the perspective of energy bands, the implantation of Ni leads to an overlap between the 2p band of O and the 3d band of Ni, inducing the formation of TM─O covalent bonds, thereby significantly activating the oxidation activity of oxygen anion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0030_R_d77521205e1388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the oxidation reaction of anionic oxygen is activated, the O<sub>2p</sub> band undergoes a split into discrete <i>σ</i>, π, π*, and <i>σ</i>* bands, with the energy difference between these bands increasing as the O─O distance decreases, that is, as the (O─O)<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>−</sup> species are oxidized. Therefore, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0003">3e</a>, the implantation of Ni tunes the band/level structure of Na<sub>2</sub>O, introducing Na vacancies and weakening the interaction of Na─O bonds, thereby effectively activating the O anionic oxidative activity. Importantly, the cleavage of Na─O bonds and formation of O─O bonds are pivotal facets of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O decomposition. During charging, the extraction of sodium facilitates the aggregation of O─O pairs. As the distance between O─O pairs decreases, conditions arise where Δct &lt; Δ<i><sup>σ</sup></i><sub>O─O</sub> and Δct &lt; Δ<sup>π</sup><sub>O─O</sub>, resulting in the generation of (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>2−</sup>, (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>−</sup>, and gaseous O<sub>2</sub> species; thus, acquiring considerable anionic oxidative capacity. In addition, we demonstrate a multi-step reaction process in the formation of O─O bonds, involving O<sup>2−</sup>, O<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup>/(O<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>), and gaseous O<sub>2</sub> intermediates. Compared to the traditional one-step reaction (4e<sup>−</sup>/O<sub>2</sub>), the stepwise formation of O─O bonds is smoother and moderate, crucial for accelerating the decomposition kinetics of Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O and reducing the reaction barrier.</p>
         
         <p>In the pursuit of enhanced electrochemical performance, presodiation electrodes were prepared by coupling varying proportions of NNO with the NVP and NNMO cathodes, respectively. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202407720-fig-0004">4a</a> left; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information show the initial charge–discharge curves for the pristine NVP and NVP-<i>n</i> wt% NNO (<i>n</i> = 5% and 10%) cathodes. The pristine NVP, along with NVP-5wt% Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O and NVP-10wt% NNO electrodes, exhibited initial charge/discharge specific capacities of 103.0/97.9 mAh/g<sub>NVP</sub>, 130.6/98.7 mAh/g<sub>NVP</sub>, and 143.2/99.8 mAh/g<sub>NVP</sub>, respectively. In addition, compared to the NNMO electrode, the cathode with the addition of 5 wt% and 10 wt% NNO presodiation agent also showed significant improvements in initial charge capacity, reaching 102 mAh/g<sub>NNMO</sub> and 119 mAh/g<sub>NNMO</sub>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0004">4a</a> right). Analysis of the differential capacity versus voltage (dQ/dV) curves revealed an augmentation in oxidation peak areas with increasing NNO additive content, predominantly concentrated within the voltage range of 3.3–4.3&nbsp;V, corresponding to the decomposition range of NNO (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Compared to the pristine NVP electrode, the NVP-5 wt% NNO and NVP-10 wt% NNO electrodes evinced a substantial augmentation in initial charge capacity by 26.8% and 39.0%, respectively. Further, the specific capacities of the NVP electrode, NVP-5 wt% NNO, and NVP-10 wt% NNO electrodes remained consistent at 71 mAh/g<sub>NVP</sub>, with a capacity retention of ≈78% after 500 cycles at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0004">4b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the rate and long-cycle performance results of NVP and NNMO after presodiation indicate that the NNO presodiation agent had a certain universality for different cathode materials and did not adversely affect the electrical performance of the cell (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15–S18</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202407720-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1d599460-6192-4fcf-a3fb-6266f1ed25dd/adma202407720-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1d599460-6192-4fcf-a3fb-6266f1ed25dd/adma202407720-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/50421603-b47f-4804-9ce5-645f402fceff/adma202407720-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1d599460-6192-4fcf-a3fb-6266f1ed25dd/adma202407720-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407720-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407720" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance after presodiation. a) Initial charge profiles of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) and Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMO) with <i>n</i> wt % (<i>n</i> = 0%, 5%, and 10%) NNO presodiation agent cathodes at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the range from 2.5 to 4.3&nbsp;V and from 2.0 to 4.15&nbsp;V, respectively. b) Cycling performance of NVP without or with NNO presodiation agent cathodes at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the range from 2.5 to 4.3&nbsp;V. Inset: the bar chart corresponds to the charge and discharge capacity of the initial cycle at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of HC||NVP without or with 10&nbsp;wt% NNO presodiation agent coin-type full-cell at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (1 st) and 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (5–50 th) in the range from 1.0 to 4.2&nbsp;V. d) Cycling performance of HC||NVP without or with 10&nbsp;wt% NNO presodiation agent coin-type full-cell in the range from 1.0 to 4.2&nbsp;V. e) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of HC||NNMO without or with 10&nbsp;wt% NNO presodiation agent coin-type full-cell at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (1st) and 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (5–50 th) in the range from 0.5 to 4.0&nbsp;V. f) Cycling performance of HC||NNMO without or with 10&nbsp;wt% NNO presodiation agent coin-type full-cell in the range from 0.5 to 4.0&nbsp;V.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Further, the utilization of NNO presodiation agent in coin-type full-cell of HC||NVP and HC||NNMO was investigated. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0004">4c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information, HC||NVP exhibited relatively high active sodium consumption, with an initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 72.7%. However, the addition of 10 wt% NNO to NVP could compensate for the initial loss of active sodium and boost the initial discharge capacity by ≈18 mAh/g<sub>NVP</sub> (a calculated enhancement of 23.9% based on the pristine NVP cathode). Even when fairly accounting for the quality of the presodiation agent, the initial discharge capacity of the HC||NVP full cell after presodiation would convert from 93.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> NVP to 84 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. This capacity was still higher than that of the discharge capacity ≈75.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of the pristine NVP cathode. The appearance of new peaks and the corresponding increase in oxidation peak areas could also be observed in the dQ/dV curves (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). After 100 cycles, the specific capacity of the NVP full-cell containing 10 wt% NNO remained at 66.0 mAh/g<sub>NVP</sub>, significantly higher than the 27.2% observed in the pristine NVP full-cell, with a notable capacity retention of ≈81.9% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0004">4d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). The initial discharge capacity of the P2-phase NNMO (sodium deficient phase) cathode containing 10 wt% NNO presodiation agent was measured at 74.7 mAh/g<sub>NNMO</sub>, significantly surpassing the 62.6 mAh/g<sub>NNMO</sub> observed in the pristine NNMO cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0004">4e</a>). After 100 cycles, the capacity retention of the NNMO full-cell with 10 wt% NNO reached 80.5%, yielding a specific capacity of 54.4 mAh/g<sub>NNMO</sub>, significantly higher than the 43.1 mAh/g<sub>NNMO</sub> observed in the pristine NNMO full-cell in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0004">4f</a> (an increase of 26.2%). Therefore, the appropriate addition of NNO presodiation agent in the cathode effectively mitigates the loss of initial active sodium, thereby playing a pivotal role in achieving superior performance and high energy density SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>To investigate the intricate 3D architecture and composition distribution of the electrolyte/cathode interphase influenced by the presodiation agent, we employ time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) for analyzing various cathode electrolyte interfaces (CEIs). Identified ion fragments include C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup>, C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>3</sub>OF<sup>−</sup>, and NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> (representing organic and inorganic species from solvent decomposition reactions), NaF<sup>−</sup> (representing inorganic species from fluorinated carbonate and NaPF<sub>6</sub>), POF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> (representing inorganic components from NaPF<sub>6</sub> decomposition products), and V<sup>−</sup> (representing bulk phase of cathode materials). Insights from the normalized intensity plots depict the trend of secondary ion distribution over sputtering time (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202407720-fig-0005">5a</a>). Both NVP and NVP-NNO cathode interphases exhibit a triple-layer CEI architecture. In NVP, C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup> is found in the outermost layer, and NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> predominates in the middle layer. Other fragments coexist in the innermost layer, indicating an accumulation of various constituents in a disordered manner. Similarly, NVP-NNO -exhibits a triple-layer CEI architecture, albeit with remarkable distinctions in distribution compared to NVP. Notably, NVP-NNO displays a more pronounced hierarchical arrangement, with NaF<sup>−</sup> and C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup> in the outer layer, NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> and POF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> in the middle layer, and C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup> and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>3</sub>OF<sup>−</sup> in the inner layer; thus, forming a relatively ordered CEI architecture. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0005">5b</a>, the 3D reconstruction reveals that the surface layer of NVP is mainly composed of C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup> fragments, with other organic and inorganic fragments dispersed unevenly throughout the interior. In contrast, in NVP-NNO, the surface layer primarily consists of inorganic species such as NaF<sup>−</sup> and POF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>; while, the innermost layer is predominantly comprised of relevant organic species such as C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>3</sub>OF<sup>−</sup> and C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup>. In addition, there is a noticeable increase in fluorinated species, suggesting that nucleophilic intermediates from the NNO presodiation agent readily react with fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) in the electrolyte, resulting in the formation of a more flexible organic inner layer.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202407720-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/beeb4c04-1c0c-4e50-9740-a9e488b58751/adma202407720-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/beeb4c04-1c0c-4e50-9740-a9e488b58751/adma202407720-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/18fda2f5-c5ea-427d-a053-1090b9e64e65/adma202407720-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/beeb4c04-1c0c-4e50-9740-a9e488b58751/adma202407720-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407720-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407720" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The impact of NNO on the electrode–electrolyte interface and associated perspective on presodiation agent. a) TOF-SIMS characterization of pure NVP and NVP-NNO cycled cathode electrodes after 150 cycles. The normalized depth profiles of the interface and bulk fragments illustrate the structure of CEI. b) 3D renderings of selected secondary ion fragments of different CEI. The sputtered volume is 100&nbsp;µm (length) × 100&nbsp;µm (width) × 150&nbsp;nm (height). c) TEM images of NVP-NNP after 200 cycles. The IFFT results for the surface and bulk regions are also listed in the figure. The light green-dashed ground state region is identified as NVP structure, white is defined as carbon layer region, and yellow is assessed as a CEI architecture. d) Scheme of CEI formation and changes on NVP and NVP-NNO cathodes summarized from characterization data in FEC-containing electrolytes. e) Perspectives for cathode presodiation agent.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Further, by employing the inverse fast Fourier transform (IFFT) mode, we conducted further investigations into structural distortions near the surface and bulk phases of NVP-NNO, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0005">5c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information. After cycling with NNO presodiation, both the thickness of the CEI film (≈3&nbsp;nm) and the structure stability of the bulk NVP were found to be similar to those observed in pristine NVP. This indicates that integrating NNO did not adversely affect the stability of the cathode interface and structure. In addition, D<sub>2</sub>O extraction liquid NMR was utilized to investigate the influence of nucleophilic species on interface stability (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information), offering comprehensive insights into soluble products. Electrolyte decomposition and parasitic reactions were intensified in the NVP-NNO cell. For instance, in comparison to the cycled electrolyte from the pristine NVP cell, the formation of by-products such as HF and carboxylates (formates and acetates) significantly increased in the NVP-NNO cell. This indicates a more complex/intense electrolyte decomposition process involving nucleophilic superoxide species in the NVP-NNO cell. As depicted in the schematic diagram in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0005">5d</a>, the introduction of the NNO presodiation agent unavoidably introduced intermediate products of nucleophilic species, which in turn led to electrolyte decomposition. However, the nucleophilic species also triggered reactions with FEC additives, resulting in the formation of a more tailored CEI architecture. In addition, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and galvanostatic intermittent titration (GITT) measurements confirmed that the unique CEI architecture formed by the NNO presodiation agent did not adversely affect cell impedance or Na-ion diffusion, and even, provided some improvement (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). The outermost layer predominantly consisted of inorganic indurated species such as NaF<sup>−</sup> and POF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>; while, the innermost layer was composed of C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>3</sub>OF<sup>−</sup>, the organic products establishing a smoother and more flexible shielding layer. This inner-flexible and outer-indurated multilayer distribution contributed to the formation of a more robust CEI architecture on the NVP-NNO surface.</p>
         
         <p>Drawing from the comprehensive analysis of information within this work, along with clarified reaction pathways and confronted challenges, we present advocacy perspectives on further modifying the presodiation agent (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407720-fig-0005">5e</a>): I) Decomposition kinetics. Building upon the foundation of material energy band engineering, researchers should aim to further enhance decomposition kinetics and reduce reaction overpotential through material structure design. II) Architecture stability: The decomposition of the presodiation agent would induce volume changes, leading to the migration and collapse of cathode particles, thereby compromising the stability of the electrode architecture. Therefore, researchers need to design a more optimized composite mode between the cathode particles and the presodiation agent, which is crucial to achieving a more stable construction of the cathode electrode architecture. (III) Parasitic reaction: The intermediate products or residues resulting from the decomposition of the presodiation agent (such as superoxide and peroxide species) own highly nucleophilic attack characteristics, posing a potential threat to the electrolyte. Therefore, researchers need to eliminate the intermediate products of side reactions as much as possible to facilitate their rapid conversion and expulsion. (IV) Shuttle effect. The decomposition products of the cathode presodiation agent shuttle to the anode side, affecting the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on the anode. This encompasses not only soluble organic compounds but also the more pronounced shuttle effect of TM. Consequently, researchers necessitate the modification of the separator to curb this shuttle effect, drawing inspiration from the modification techniques applied to separators in lithium–sulfur batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407720-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407720-bib-0031_R_d77521205e1753" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Energy CCS Chem. Adv. Sci.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (V) Safety issue. Sacrificial presodiation agents frequently emit gases during the formation (de-gas) process, such as O<sub>2</sub>; while, gases such as H<sub>2</sub> and CH<sub>4</sub> may also emerge on the anode electrode side. Although these occurrences are meticulously scrutinized in the industry, they are unfortunately often overlooked in laboratory settings. The generation and amalgamation of these significant volumes of gases can potentially lead to safety hazards, including explosions. Therefore, it is imperative to thoroughly expel the gases generated during the formation process through negative pressure. Based on the above suggestions and perspectives, researchers should give due attention and make improvements; it is expected to accelerate the transition of presodiation agent from laboratory research to practical industrial application.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202407720-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202407720-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we employed a transition metal (TM) implantation strategy, doping TM into the functional sites (Na sites) of Na<sub>2</sub>O and successfully synthesized NNO cathode presodiation agent. We demonstrated that Ni atom implantation and Na vacancy tuned the energy level/band structure of Na<sub>2</sub>O, enhancing the conductivity of the pristine Na<sub>2</sub>O and activating the oxidation activity of O anion. In addition, in combination with the catalytic effect of NiO, it accelerated the kinetics of Na<sub>2</sub>O decomposition. The combined effect of regulating the internal crystal structure of Na<sub>2</sub>O and the external catalysis of NiO facilitated the release of active Na and O<sub>2</sub>, significantly reducing the overpotential of Na<sub>2</sub>O decomposition, ultimately achieving a high presodiation capacity of 710 mAh/g <sub>≈ Na2O</sub> and a high decomposition rate of 82%. Synchrotron radiation characterizations proved the formation of tetrahedral Ni–O coordination due to Ni doping in the Na<sub>2</sub>O framework. As gaseous O<sub>2</sub> irreversibly escaped and active sodium was extracted, its structure underwent a fundamental evolution, transitioning from the pristine Ni–Na<sub>2</sub>O antifluorite structure to residual NiO. Further, through systematic in situ/ex-situ characterization techniques (e.g., OEMS, titration-MS, soft X-ray, etc.), we demonstrated the oxidation pathway of O anion during NNO decomposition, demonstrating the multi-step reaction process generating diverse O─O dimers in the decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>O. The effective Ni implantation and NiO surface catalysis expedited the kinetics of Na─O bond cleavage and O─O bond formation, affirming the multi-step transformation pathway of intermediate species including O<sup>2−</sup>, O<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup> (O<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>), and gaseous O<sub>2</sub>. In addition, the nucleophilic intermediate species accelerated the decomposition of the electrolyte on the one hand; while, promoting the decomposition of the electrolyte additive FEC on the other hand, constructing a fluorine-rich and flexible CEI architecture. Compared to the pristine NVP cathode, the initial charge capacity of the NVP with 10 wt% NNO cathode increased significantly by 39.0% (from 103 mAh/g<sub>NVP</sub> to 143 mAh/g<sub>NVP</sub>). In addition, the first-cycle discharge capacity of HC||NVP and HC||NNMO full-cells with 10 wt% NNO increased by 23.9% and 19.3%, respectively, without any impact on their capacity retention rate. Further, we conducted a comprehensive evaluation of the cathode presodiation agent, advocating for the establishment of standardized research paradigms in future studies to truly realize the efficient utilization of cathode presodiation/prelithiation agent and significantly improve the energy density of sodium/lithium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202407720-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202407720-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>

         </section>
